The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lincoln_Park,_Denver,_Colorado" title="Lincoln Park, Denver, Colorado">Santa Fe Arts District</a> on Santa Fe Drive</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SakuraSquare.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2177" data-file-width="2678" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/SakuraSquare.JPG/220px-SakuraSquare.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/SakuraSquare.JPG/330px-SakuraSquare.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/SakuraSquare.JPG/440px-SakuraSquare.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<p>While Denver may not be as recognized for historical musical prominence as some other American cities, it still manages to have a very active pop, <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jam_band" title="Jam band">jam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Folk_music" title="Folk music">folk</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_classical_music" title="European classical music">classical music</a> scene, which has nurtured several artists and genres to regional, national, and even international attention. Of particular note is Denver's importance in the <a href="/wiki/Folk_music" title="Folk music">folk scene</a> of the 1960s and 1970s. Well-known folk artists such as <a href="/wiki/Bob_Dylan" title="Bob Dylan">Bob Dylan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judy_Collins" title="Judy Collins">Judy Collins</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Denver" title="John Denver">John Denver</a> lived in Denver at various points during this time, and performed at local clubs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[95]</a></sup> Also, three members of the widely popular group <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth,_Wind,_and_Fire" title="Earth, Wind, and Fire">Earth, Wind, and Fire</a> are from Denver. More recent Denver-based artists include <a href="/wiki/The_Lumineers" title="The Lumineers">The Lumineers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Air_Dubai" title="Air Dubai">Air Dubai</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Fray" title="The Fray">The Fray</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flobots" title="Flobots">Flobots</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cephalic_Carnage" title="Cephalic Carnage">Cephalic Carnage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Axe_Murder_Boyz" title="Axe Murder Boyz">Axe Murder Boyz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deuce_Mob" title="Deuce Mob">Deuce Mob</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Five_Iron_Frenzy" title="Five Iron Frenzy">Five Iron Frenzy</a>.</p>
<p>Because of its proximity to the mountains and generally sunny weather, Denver has gained a reputation as being a very active, outdoor-oriented city. Many Denver residents spend the weekends in the mountains; skiing in the winter and hiking, climbing, kayaking, and camping in the summer.</p>
<p>Denver and surrounding cities are home to a large number of local and national breweries. Many restaurants in the region have on-site breweries, and some of the larger brewers offer tours, including <a href="/wiki/Coors_Brewing_Company" title="Coors Brewing Company">Coors</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Belgium_Brewing_Company" title="New Belgium Brewing Company">New Belgium Brewing Company</a>. The city also welcomes visitors from around the world when it hosts the annual <a href="/wiki/Great_American_Beer_Festival" title="Great American Beer Festival">Great American Beer Festival</a> each fall.</p>
<p>Denver used to be a major trading center for beef and livestock when ranchers would drive (or later transport) cattle to the Denver Union Stockyards for sale. As a celebration of that history, for more than a century Denver has hosted the annual <a href="/wiki/National_Western_Stock_Show" title="National Western Stock Show">National Western Stock Show</a>, attracting as many as 10,000 animals and 700,000 attendees. The show is held every January at the National Western Complex northeast of downtown.</p>
<p>Denver has one of the country's largest populations of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexican_American" title="Mexican American">Mexican Americans</a> and hosts four large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mexican_American" title="Mexican American">Mexican American</a> celebrations: <a href="/wiki/Cinco_de_Mayo" title="Cinco de Mayo">Cinco de Mayo</a> (with over 500,000 attendees),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[96]</a></sup> in May, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grito_de_Dolores" title="Grito de Dolores">El Grito de la Independencia</a>, in September, the annual <a href="/wiki/Lowrider" title="Lowrider">Lowrider</a> show, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dia_De_Los_Muertos" title="Dia De Los Muertos">Dia De Los Muertos</a> art shows/events in North Denver's <a href="/wiki/Highland,_Denver" title="Highland, Denver">Highland</a> neighborhood, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lincoln_Park,_Denver,_Colorado" title="Lincoln Park, Denver, Colorado">Lincoln Park</a> neighborhood in the original section of West Denver.</p>
<p>Denver is also famous for its dedication to <a href="/wiki/New_Mexican_cuisine" title="New Mexican cuisine">New Mexican cuisine</a> and the chile. It's best known for its green and red chile sauce, <a href="/wiki/Cuisine_of_the_Southwestern_United_States" title="Cuisine of the Southwestern United States">Colorado burrito</a>, Southwest (Denver) <a href="/wiki/Omelette" title="Omelette">omelette</a>, breakfast burrito, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chiles_rellenos" title="Chiles rellenos">chiles rellenos</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tamale" title="Tamale">tamales</a>. Denver is also well known for other types of food such as <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountain_oysters" title="Rocky Mountain oysters">Rocky Mountain oysters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rainbow_trout" title="Rainbow trout">rainbow trout</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Denver_sandwich" title="Denver sandwich">Denver sandwich</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Dragon_Boat_Festival" title="Dragon Boat Festival">Dragon Boat Festival</a> in July, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moon_Festival" title="Moon Festival">Moon Festival</a> in September and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_New_Year" title="Chinese New Year">Chinese New Year</a> are annual events in Denver for the Chinese and Asian residents. Chinese hot pot (huo guo) and <a href="/wiki/Korean_cuisine" title="Korean cuisine">Korean</a> BBQ restaurants have been growing in popularity. The Denver area has 2 Chinese newspapers, the <a class="external text" href="http://www.chineseamericanpost.com/" rel="nofollow">Chinese American Post</a> and the <a class="external text" href="http://www.cocnews.com/" rel="nofollow">Colorado Chinese News</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>Denver is the setting for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bill_Engvall_Show" title="The Bill Engvall Show">The Bill Engvall Show</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/The_Real_World:_Denver" title="The Real World: Denver">the 18th season</a> of MTV's <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Real_World" title="The Real World">The Real World</a></i>. It was also the setting for the prime time drama <i><a href="/wiki/Dynasty_(TV_series)" title="Dynasty (TV series)">Dynasty</a></i> from 1981 to 1989 (although the show was mostly filmed in Los Angeles). From 1998 to 2002 the city's <a href="/wiki/Alameda_East_Veterinary_Hospital" title="Alameda East Veterinary Hospital">Alameda East Veterinary Hospital</a> was home to the <a href="/wiki/Animal_Planet" title="Animal Planet">Animal Planet</a> series <i><a href="/wiki/Emergency_Vets" title="Emergency Vets">Emergency Vets</a></i>, which spun off three one-off documentary specials and the current Animal Planet series <i><a href="/wiki/E-Vet_Interns" title="E-Vet Interns">E-Vet Interns</a></i>. The city is also the setting for the <a href="/wiki/Disney_Channel" title="Disney Channel">Disney Channel Original TV Show</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Good_Luck_Charlie" title="Good Luck Charlie">Good Luck Charlie</a></i>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Sports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sports_in_Denver" title="Sports in Denver">Sports in Denver</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Invesco_Field_at_Mile_High.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Invesco_Field_at_Mile_High.jpg/200px-Invesco_Field_at_Mile_High.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Invesco_Field_at_Mile_High.jpg/300px-Invesco_Field_at_Mile_High.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Invesco_Field_at_Mile_High.jpg/400px-Invesco_Field_at_Mile_High.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>

Dicks Sporting Goods park during a game between the Colorado Rapids and Los Angeles Galaxy (November 2016)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Denver is home to a variety of sports teams and is one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._cities_with_teams_from_four_major_sports" title="U.S. cities with teams from four major sports">U.S. cities with teams from four major sports</a> (the Denver metro area is the smallest metropolitan area to have a team in all four major sports). The <a href="/wiki/Denver_Broncos" title="Denver Broncos">Denver Broncos</a> of the <a href="/wiki/National_Football_League" title="National Football League">National Football League</a> have drawn crowds of over 70,000 since their origins in the early 1960s, and continue to draw fans today to their current home <a href="/wiki/Sports_Authority_Field_at_Mile_High" title="Sports Authority Field at Mile High">Sports Authority Field at Mile High</a>. The Broncos have sold out every home game (except for strike-replacement games) since 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup> The Broncos have advanced to eight <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl" title="Super Bowl">Super Bowls</a> and won back-to-back titles in 1997 and 1998, and won again in 2015.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Rockies" title="Colorado Rockies">Colorado Rockies</a> were created as an expansion franchise in 1993 and <a href="/wiki/Coors_Field" title="Coors Field">Coors Field</a> opened in 1995. The Rockies advanced to the playoffs that year, but were eliminated in the first round. In 2007, they advanced to the playoffs as a wild-card entrant, won the NL Championship Series, and brought the <a href="/wiki/World_Series" title="World Series">World Series</a> to Denver for the first time but were swept in four games by the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Red_Sox" title="Boston Red Sox">Boston Red Sox</a>.</p>
<p>Denver has been home to two National Hockey League teams. The <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Rockies_(NHL)" title="Colorado Rockies (NHL)">Colorado Rockies</a> played from 1976 to 1982, but became the <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey_Devils" title="New Jersey Devils">New Jersey Devils</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Avalanche" title="Colorado Avalanche">Colorado Avalanche</a> joined in 1995, after relocating from Quebec City. While in Denver, they have won two <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Cup" title="Stanley Cup">Stanley Cups</a> in 1996 and in 2001. The <a href="/wiki/Denver_Nuggets" title="Denver Nuggets">Denver Nuggets</a> joined the <a href="/wiki/American_Basketball_Association" title="American Basketball Association">American Basketball Association</a> in 1967 and the <a href="/wiki/National_Basketball_Association" title="National Basketball Association">National Basketball Association</a> in 1976. The Avalanche and Nuggets have played at <a href="/wiki/Pepsi_Center" title="Pepsi Center">Pepsi Center</a> since 1999. The Major League Soccer team <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Rapids" title="Colorado Rapids">Colorado Rapids</a> play in <a href="/wiki/Dick%27s_Sporting_Goods_Park" title="Dick's Sporting Goods Park">Dick's Sporting Goods Park</a>, an 18,000 seat <a href="/wiki/Soccer-specific_stadium" title="Soccer-specific stadium">soccer-specific stadium</a> opened for the 2007 MLS season, located in the Denver suburb of Commerce City.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[99]</a></sup> The Rapids won the MLS Cup in 2010.</p>

<p>Denver has several additional professional teams. In 2006 Denver established a Major League Lacrosse team, the <a href="/wiki/Denver_Outlaws" title="Denver Outlaws">Denver Outlaws</a>. They play in <a href="/wiki/Sports_Authority_Field_at_Mile_High" title="Sports Authority Field at Mile High">Sports Authority Field at Mile High</a>. In 2006, the Denver Outlaws won the Western Conference Championship. The <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Mammoth" title="Colorado Mammoth">Colorado Mammoth</a> of the National Lacrosse League play at the Pepsi Center. The <a href="/wiki/Denver_Stampede" title="Denver Stampede">Denver Stampede</a> plays at <a href="/wiki/Infinity_Park" title="Infinity Park">Infinity Park</a>.</p>
<p>Denver submitted the winning bid to host the <a href="/wiki/1976_Winter_Olympics" title="1976 Winter Olympics">1976 Winter Olympics</a>, but subsequently withdrew, giving it the dubious distinction of being the only city to back out after winning a bid to host the Olympics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1970Olympics_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-1970Olympics-43">[43]</a></sup> Denver and Colorado Springs hosted the <a href="/wiki/1962_World_Ice_Hockey_Championships" title="1962 World Ice Hockey Championships">1962 World Ice Hockey Championships</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Parks_and_recreation">Parks and recreation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Parks and recreation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As of 2006<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Denver&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Denver had over 200 parks, from small mini-parks all over the city to the giant 314 acres (1.27 km<sup>2</sup>) <a href="/wiki/City_Park,_Denver" title="City Park, Denver">City Park</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[105]</a></sup> Denver also has 29 recreation centers providing places and programming for resident's recreation and relaxation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[106]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Denver_16th_st.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="2400" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Denver_16th_st.jpg/220px-Denver_16th_st.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Denver_16th_st.jpg/330px-Denver_16th_st.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Denver_16th_st.jpg/440px-Denver_16th_st.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Denver_16th_st.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Chess players on the <a href="/wiki/16th_Street_Mall" title="16th Street Mall">16th Street Mall</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Many of Denver's parks were acquired from state lands in the late 19th and early 20th centuries. This coincided with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_Beautiful" title="City Beautiful">City Beautiful</a> movement, and Denver mayor <a href="/wiki/Robert_W._Speer" title="Robert W. Speer">Robert Speer</a> (1904–12 and 1916–18) set out to expand and beautify the city's parks. Reinhard Schuetze was the city's first <a href="/wiki/Landscape_architect" title="Landscape architect">landscape architect</a>, and he brought his German-educated landscaping genius to <a href="/wiki/Washington_Park,_Denver" title="Washington Park, Denver">Washington Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cheesman_Park,_Denver" title="Cheesman Park, Denver">Cheesman Park</a>, and <a href="/wiki/City_Park,_Denver" title="City Park, Denver">City Park</a> among others. Speer used Schuetze as well as other landscape architects such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frederick_Law_Olmsted,_Jr." title="Frederick Law Olmsted, Jr.">Frederick Law Olmsted, Jr.</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saco_Rienk_DeBoer" title="Saco Rienk DeBoer">Saco Rienk DeBoer</a> to design not only parks such as <a href="/wiki/Civic_Center,_Denver" title="Civic Center, Denver">Civic Center Park</a>, but many city parkways and tree-lawns. All of this greenery was fed with <a href="/wiki/South_Platte_River" title="South Platte River">South Platte River</a> water diverted through the city ditch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[107]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cheesman_Park_3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Cheesman_Park_3.jpg/220px-Cheesman_Park_3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Cheesman_Park_3.jpg/330px-Cheesman_Park_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Cheesman_Park_3.jpg/440px-Cheesman_Park_3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cheesman_Park_3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cheesman_Park" title="Cheesman Park">Cheesman Park</a> started as a cemetery.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CityParkDen.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2160" data-file-width="3840" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/CityParkDen.JPG/220px-CityParkDen.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/CityParkDen.JPG/330px-CityParkDen.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/CityParkDen.JPG/440px-CityParkDen.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CityParkDen.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
City Park</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In addition to the parks within Denver itself, the city acquired land for <a href="/wiki/Denver_Mountain_Parks" title="Denver Mountain Parks">mountain parks</a> starting in the 1911s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[108]</a></sup> Over the years, Denver has acquired, built and maintained approximately 14,000 acres (57 km<sup>2</sup>) of mountain parks, including <a href="/wiki/Red_Rocks_Park" title="Red Rocks Park">Red Rocks Park</a>, which is known for its scenery and musical history revolving around the unique <a href="/wiki/Red_Rocks_Amphitheatre" title="Red Rocks Amphitheatre">Red Rocks Amphitheatre</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[110]</a></sup> Denver also owns the mountain on which the <a href="/wiki/Winter_Park_Resort" title="Winter Park Resort">Winter Park Resort</a> ski area is operated in <a href="/wiki/Grand_County,_Colorado" title="Grand County, Colorado">Grand County</a>, 67 miles (110 km) west of Denver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[111]</a></sup> City parks are important places for both Denverites and visitors, inciting controversy with every change. Denver continues to grow its park system with the development of many new parks along the Platte River through the city, and with Central Park and <a href="/wiki/Bluff_Lake_Nature_Center" title="Bluff Lake Nature Center">Bluff Lake Nature Center</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Stapleton_International_Airport" title="Stapleton International Airport">Stapleton</a> neighborhood redevelopment. All of these parks are important gathering places for residents and allow what was once a dry plain to be lush, active, and green. Denver is also home to a large network of public <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Community_gardens" title="Community gardens">community gardens</a>, most of which are managed by <a href="/wiki/Denver_Urban_Gardens" title="Denver Urban Gardens">Denver Urban Gardens</a>, a non-profit organization.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dmp.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1445" data-file-width="2144" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Dmp.JPG/220px-Dmp.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Dmp.JPG/330px-Dmp.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Dmp.JPG/440px-Dmp.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dmp.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Genesee_Park_(Colorado)" title="Genesee Park (Colorado)">Genesee Park</a> is the largest of the Denver Mountain Parks.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Since 1974, Denver and the surrounding jurisdictions have rehabilitated the urban South Platte River and its tributaries for recreational use by hikers and cyclists. The main stem of the South Platte River Greenway runs along the South Platte from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chatfield_Dam" title="Chatfield Dam">Chatfield Reservoir</a> 35 miles (56 km) into Adams County in the north. The Greenway project is recognized as one of the best urban reclamation projects in the U.S., winning, for example, the Silver Medal <a href="/wiki/Rudy_Bruner_Award_for_Urban_Excellence" title="Rudy Bruner Award for Urban Excellence">Rudy Bruner Award for Urban Excellence</a> in 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruner_Foundation_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bruner_Foundation-114">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>In its 2013 ParkScore ranking, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trust_for_Public_Land" title="Trust for Public Land">The Trust for Public Land</a>, a national land conservation organization, reported that Denver had the 17th best park system among the 50 most populous U.S. cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[113]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Denver" title="Government of Denver">Government of Denver</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Denver_City_Hall_1941.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Denver_City_Hall_1941.jpg/220px-Denver_City_Hall_1941.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Denver_City_Hall_1941.jpg/330px-Denver_City_Hall_1941.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Denver_City_Hall_1941.jpg/440px-Denver_City_Hall_1941.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Denver_City_Hall_1941.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Denver City and County Building (circa 1941), looking west.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DenCtyHallChristmas1955.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="847" data-file-width="1798" height="104" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/DenCtyHallChristmas1955.jpg/220px-DenCtyHallChristmas1955.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/DenCtyHallChristmas1955.jpg/330px-DenCtyHallChristmas1955.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/DenCtyHallChristmas1955.jpg/440px-DenCtyHallChristmas1955.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DenCtyHallChristmas1955.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Denver City Hall Christmas lights, 1955</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coloradocapitolhill2.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1960" data-file-width="2982" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Coloradocapitolhill2.JPG/220px-Coloradocapitolhill2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Coloradocapitolhill2.JPG/330px-Coloradocapitolhill2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Coloradocapitolhill2.JPG/440px-Coloradocapitolhill2.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coloradocapitolhill2.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Colorado_State_Capitol" title="Colorado State Capitol">Colorado State Capitol</a> looking east.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Denver is a <a href="/wiki/Consolidated_city-county" title="Consolidated city-county">consolidated city-county</a> with a mayor elected on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nonpartisan" title="Nonpartisan">nonpartisan</a> ballot, a 13-member <a href="/wiki/City_council" title="City council">city council</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Auditor" title="Auditor">auditor</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Denver_City_Council" title="Denver City Council">Denver City Council</a> is elected from 11 districts with two at-large council-members and is responsible for passing and changing all laws, resolutions, and ordinances, usually after a public hearing, and can also call for misconduct investigations of Denver's departmental officials. All elected officials have four-year terms, with a maximum of three terms. The current mayor is <a href="/wiki/Michael_Hancock_(Colorado_politician)" title="Michael Hancock (Colorado politician)">Michael Hancock</a>.</p>
<p>Denver has a strong mayor/weak city council government. The mayor can approve or veto any ordinances or <a href="/wiki/Resolution_(law)" title="Resolution (law)">resolutions</a> approved by the council, makes sure all contracts with the city are kept and performed, signs all bonds and contracts, is responsible for the city budget, and can appoint people to various city departments, organizations, and commissions. However, the council can override the mayor's veto with a nine out of thirteen member vote, and the city budget must be approved and can be changed by a simple majority vote of the council. The auditor checks all expenditures and may refuse to allow specific ones, usually based on financial reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Denver Department of Safety oversees three branches: the <a href="/wiki/Denver_Police_Department" title="Denver Police Department">Denver Police Department</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denver_Fire_Department" title="Denver Fire Department">Denver Fire Department</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Denver_Sheriff_Department" title="Denver Sheriff Department">Denver Sheriff Department</a>. The Denver County Court is an integrated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colorado_County_Courts" title="Colorado County Courts">Colorado County Court</a> and Municipal Court and is managed by Denver instead of the state.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While Denver elections are non-partisan, <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democrats</a> have long dominated the city's politics; most citywide officials are known to be Democrats. The mayor's office has been occupied by a Democrat since the 1963 municipal election. All of the city's seats in the state legislature are held by Democrats.</p>
<p>In federal elections, Denverites also tend to vote for Democratic candidates, voting for the Democratic Presidential nominee in every election since 1960, excluding 1972 and 1980. At the federal level, Denver is the heart of <a href="/wiki/Colorado%27s_1st_congressional_district" title="Colorado's 1st congressional district">Colorado's 1st congressional district</a>, which includes all of Denver and parts of <a href="/wiki/Arapahoe_County,_Colorado" title="Arapahoe County, Colorado">Arapahoe County</a>. It is currently represented by Democrat <a href="/wiki/Diana_DeGette" title="Diana DeGette">Diana DeGette</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Benjamin_F._Stapleton" title="Benjamin F. Stapleton">Benjamin F. Stapleton</a> was the mayor of Denver, Colorado, for two periods, the first from 1923 to 1931 and the second from 1935 to 1947. Stapleton was responsible for many civic improvements during his term, notably during his second stint as mayor when he had access to funds and manpower from the <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a>. During this time, the park system was considerably expanded and the Civic Center completed. His signature project was the construction of Denver Municipal Airport, which began in 1929 amidst heavy criticism. It was later renamed <a href="/wiki/Stapleton_International_Airport" title="Stapleton International Airport">Stapleton International Airport</a> in his honor. Today, the airport no longer stands, but has been replaced by a neighborhood also named Stapleton. Stapleton Street continues to bear his name.</p>
<p>During the 1960s and 1970s, Denver was one of the epicenters of the <a href="/wiki/Chicano_Movement" title="Chicano Movement">Chicano Movement</a>. The boxer-turned-activist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rodolfo_%22Corky%22_Gonzales" title='Rodolfo "Corky" Gonzales'>Rodolfo "Corky" Gonzales</a> formed an organization called the Crusade for Justice, which battled police brutality, fought for bilingual education, and, most notably, hosted the First National Chicano Youth Liberation Conference in March 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>In recent years, Denver has taken a stance on helping people who are or become <a href="/wiki/Homelessness" title="Homelessness">homeless</a>, particularly under the administrations of mayors <a href="/wiki/John_Hickenlooper" title="John Hickenlooper">John Hickenlooper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wellington_Webb" title="Wellington Webb">Wellington Webb</a>. At a rate of 19 homeless per 10,000 residents in 2011 as compared to 50 or more per 10,000 residents for the four metro areas with the highest rate of homelessness,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[116]</a></sup> Denver's homeless population and rate of homeless are both considerably lower than many other major cities. However, residents of the city streets suffer Denver winters - which, although mild and dry much of the time, can have brief periods of extremely cold temperatures and snow.</p>
<p>In 2005, Denver became the first major city in the U.S. to vote to make the private possession of less than an ounce of <a href="/wiki/Cannabis_(drug)" title="Cannabis (drug)">marijuana</a> legal for adults 21 and older.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DenverPot_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-DenverPot-119">[117]</a></sup> The city voted 53.5 percent in favor of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marijuana_legalization" title="Marijuana legalization">marijuana legalization</a> measure, which, as then-mayor John Hickenlooper pointed out, was without effect, because the city cannot usurp state law, which at that time treated marijuana possession in much the same way as a speeding ticket, with fines of up to $100 and no jail time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DenverPot_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-DenverPot-119">[117]</a></sup> Denver passed an initiative in the fourth quarter of 2007 requiring the mayor to appoint an 11-member review panel to monitor the city's compliance with the 2005 ordinance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[118]</a></sup> In 2012, <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Amendment_64" title="Colorado Amendment 64">Colorado Amendment 64</a> was signed into law by Governor John Hickenlooper and at the beginning of 2014 Colorado became the first state to allow the sale of marijuana for recreational use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>Former Denver mayor John Hickenlooper was a member of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mayors_Against_Illegal_Guns_Coalition" title="Mayors Against Illegal Guns Coalition">Mayors Against Illegal Guns Coalition</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[120]</a></sup> an organization formed in 2006 and co-chaired by New York City mayor <a href="/wiki/Michael_Bloomberg" title="Michael Bloomberg">Michael Bloomberg</a> and Boston mayor <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Menino" title="Thomas Menino">Thomas Menino</a>.</p>
<p>Denver hosted the <a href="/wiki/2008_Democratic_National_Convention" title="2008 Democratic National Convention">2008 Democratic National Convention</a>, which was the centennial of the city's first hosting of the landmark 1908 convention. It also hosted the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G8" title="G8">G7</a> (now G8) summit between June 20 and 22 in 1997 and the 2000 National Convention of the <a href="/wiki/Green_Party_of_the_United_States" title="Green Party of the United States">Green Party</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>On October 31, 2011 it was announced that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_University_of_Denver" title="The University of Denver">The University of Denver</a> in Denver was selected as the host of the first of three 2012 presidential debates to be held on October 3, 2012.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxes">Taxes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Taxes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The City and County of Denver levies an Occupational Privilege Tax (OPT or Head Tax) on employers and employees.</p>
<ul>
<li>If any employee performs work in the city limits and is paid over $500 for that work in a single month, the employee and employer are both liable for the OPT regardless of where the main business office is located or headquartered.</li>
<li>The employer is liable for $4 per employee per month and the employee is liable for $5.75 per month.</li>
<li>It is the employer's responsibility to withhold, remit, and file the OPT returns. If an employer does not comply, the employer can be held liable for both portions of the OPT as well as penalties and interest.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ritchie_Center_sunrise_2006.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Ritchie_Center_sunrise_2006.jpg/220px-Ritchie_Center_sunrise_2006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Ritchie_Center_sunrise_2006.jpg/330px-Ritchie_Center_sunrise_2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Ritchie_Center_sunrise_2006.jpg/440px-Ritchie_Center_sunrise_2006.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ritchie_Center_sunrise_2006.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Ritchie Center at University of Denver</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_higher_education_institutions_in_Denver" title="List of higher education institutions in Denver">List of higher education institutions in Denver</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Denver_Public_Schools" title="Denver Public Schools">Denver Public Schools</a> (DPS) is the public school system in Denver. It currently educates about 73,000 students in 73 elementary schools, 15 K-8 schools, 17 <a href="/wiki/Secondary_school" title="Secondary school">middle schools</a>, 14 high schools, and 19 <a href="/wiki/Charter_school" title="Charter school">charter schools</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[123]</a></sup> The first school of what is now DPS was a log cabin that opened in 1859 on the corner of 12th Street between Market and Larimer Streets. The district boundaries are coextensive with the city limits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schooldistrict_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schooldistrict-126">[124]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cherry_Creek_School_District" title="Cherry Creek School District">Cherry Creek School District</a> serves some areas with Denver postal addresses that are outside the city limits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schooldistrict_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schooldistrict-126">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aboutus_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aboutus-127">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>Denver's many colleges and universities range in age and study programs. Three major public schools constitute the <a href="/wiki/Auraria_Campus" title="Auraria Campus">Auraria Campus</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Colorado_Denver" title="University of Colorado Denver">University of Colorado Denver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_State_University_of_Denver" title="Metropolitan State University of Denver">Metropolitan State University of Denver</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Community_College_of_Denver" title="Community College of Denver">Community College of Denver</a>. The private <a href="/wiki/University_of_Denver" title="University of Denver">University of Denver</a> was the first institution of higher learning in the city and was founded in 1864. Other prominent Denver higher education institutions include <a href="/wiki/Johnson_%26_Wales_University" title="Johnson &amp; Wales University">Johnson &amp; Wales University</a>, Catholic (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesuit" title="Jesuit">Jesuit</a>) <a href="/wiki/Regis_University" title="Regis University">Regis University</a> and the city has Roman Catholic and Jewish institutions, as well as a health sciences school. In addition to those schools within the city, there are a number of schools located throughout the surrounding metro area.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Media_in_Denver" title="Media in Denver">Media in Denver</a></div>
<p>The Denver Metropolitan Area is served by a variety of media outlets in print, radio, television, and the Internet.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Television_stations">Television stations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Television stations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Denver is the 16th-largest market in the country for television, according to the 2009–2010 <a class="external text" href="http://blog.nielsen.com/nielsenwire/media_entertainment/1149-million-us-television-homes-estimated-for-2009-2010-season" rel="nofollow">rankings</a> from <a href="/wiki/Nielsen_Media_Research" title="Nielsen Media Research">Nielsen Media Research</a>.</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/KWGN-TV" title="KWGN-TV">KWGN-TV</a>, channel 2, is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_CW_Television_Network" title="The CW Television Network">CW</a> affiliate owned by <a href="/wiki/Tribune_Broadcasting" title="Tribune Broadcasting">Tribune Broadcasting</a>. Tribune also owns <a href="/wiki/KDVR" title="KDVR">KDVR</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Fox_Broadcasting_Company" title="Fox Broadcasting Company">Fox</a> affiliate on channel 31, and KWGN is controlled by KDVR management. KWGN is Colorado's first television station, signing on the air in July 1952.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/KCNC-TV" title="KCNC-TV">KCNC-TV</a>, channel 4, is a <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Owned_and_operated_station" title="Owned and operated station">owned and operated station</a>.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KRMA-TV" title="KRMA-TV">KRMA-TV</a>, channel 6, is the flagship outlet of <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountain_PBS" title="Rocky Mountain PBS">Rocky Mountain PBS</a>, a statewide network of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_Broadcasting_Service" title="Public Broadcasting Service">Public Broadcasting Service</a> stations. Programming on KRMA is rebroadcast to four other stations throughout Colorado.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/KMGH-TV" title="KMGH-TV">KMGH-TV</a>, channel 7, is an <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">ABC</a> affiliate owned by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E.W._Scripps_Company" title="E.W. Scripps Company">E.W. Scripps Company</a>, previously owned by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/McGraw-Hill" title="McGraw-Hill">McGraw-Hill</a> company from 1972 to January 2012.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KUSA-TV" title="KUSA-TV">KUSA-TV</a>, channel 9, is an <a href="/wiki/NBC" title="NBC">NBC</a> affiliate, owned by <a href="/wiki/Tegna,_Inc." title="Tegna, Inc.">Tegna, Inc.</a>. TEGNA also owns <a href="/wiki/KTVD" title="KTVD">KTVD</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/My_Network_TV" title="My Network TV">My Network TV</a> affiliate on channel 20.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/KBDI-TV" title="KBDI-TV">KBDI-TV</a>, channel 12, is Denver's secondary PBS affiliate.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/KDEN-TV" title="KDEN-TV">KDEN-TV</a>, channel 25, is a <a href="/wiki/Telemundo" title="Telemundo">Telemundo</a>-owned station.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/KPJR-TV" title="KPJR-TV">KPJR-TV</a>, channel 38, is a <a href="/wiki/Trinity_Broadcasting_Network" title="Trinity Broadcasting Network">Trinity Broadcasting Network</a>-owned station.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/KCEC_(TV)" title="KCEC (TV)">KCEC</a>, channel 50, is the <a href="/wiki/Univision" title="Univision">Univision</a> affiliate.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/KETD" title="KETD">KETD</a>, channel 53, is a Christian station owned by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LeSea_Broadcasting" title="LeSea Broadcasting">LeSea Broadcasting</a> group.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radio_stations">Radio stations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Radio stations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Denver is also served by over 40 AM and FM radio stations, covering a wide variety of formats and styles. Denver-Boulder radio is the No. 19 market in the United States, according to the Spring 2011 <a class="external text" href="http://www.arbitron.com/radio_stations/mm001050.asp" rel="nofollow">Arbitron</a> ranking (up from No. 20 in Fall 2009). For a list of radio stations, see <a href="/wiki/List_of_radio_stations_in_Colorado" title="List of radio stations in Colorado">Radio Stations in Colorado</a></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Print">Print</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Print">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After a continued rivalry between Denver's two main newspapers, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Denver_Post" title="Denver Post">Denver Post</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountain_News" title="Rocky Mountain News">Rocky Mountain News</a></i>, the papers merged operations in 2001 under a Joint Operating Agreement which formed the Denver Newspaper Agency<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[126]</a></sup> until February 2009 when <a href="/wiki/E._W._Scripps_Company" title="E. W. Scripps Company">E. W. Scripps Company</a>, the owner of the Rocky Mountain News closed the paper. There are also several alternative or localized newspapers published in Denver, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Westword" title="Westword">Westword</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Out_Front_Colorado" title="Out Front Colorado">Out Front Colorado</a></i>. Denver is home to multiple regional magazines such as <i><a href="/wiki/5280" title="5280">5280</a></i>, which takes its name from the city's mile-high (5,280 feet or 1,609 meters) elevation.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Transportation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:543px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sunrise_Over_Denver_Skyline.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1688" data-file-width="3672" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Sunrise_Over_Denver_Skyline.jpg/541px-Sunrise_Over_Denver_Skyline.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Sunrise_Over_Denver_Skyline.jpg/812px-Sunrise_Over_Denver_Skyline.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Sunrise_Over_Denver_Skyline.jpg/1082px-Sunrise_Over_Denver_Skyline.jpg 2x" width="541"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sunrise_Over_Denver_Skyline.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dawn over downtown Denver and the <a href="/wiki/Front_Range" title="Front Range">Front Range</a> to the south.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="City_streets">City streets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: City streets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Street_system_of_Denver" title="Street system of Denver">Street system of Denver</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Colfaxatbroadway.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1217" data-file-width="1070" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Colfaxatbroadway.JPG/170px-Colfaxatbroadway.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Colfaxatbroadway.JPG/255px-Colfaxatbroadway.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Colfaxatbroadway.JPG/340px-Colfaxatbroadway.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Colfaxatbroadway.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Colfax Avenue at Broadway, where the downtown street grid and the "normal" city grid meet. Colfax Avenue carries <a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_40_in_Colorado" title="U.S. Route 40 in Colorado">U.S. Highway 40</a> through Denver.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most of Denver has a straightforward <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Street_grid" title="Street grid">street grid</a> oriented to the four <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_direction" title="Cardinal direction">cardinal directions</a>. Blocks are usually identified in hundreds from the median streets, identified as "00", which are Broadway (the east–west median, running north–south) and Ellsworth Avenue (the north–south median, running east–west). <a href="/wiki/Colfax_Avenue" title="Colfax Avenue">Colfax Avenue</a>, a major east–west artery through Denver, is 15 blocks (1500) north of the median. Avenues north of Ellsworth are numbered (with the exception of Colfax Avenue and several others, such as Martin Luther King, Jr. Blvd and Montview Blvd.), while avenues south of Ellsworth are named.</p>
<p>There is also an older downtown grid system that was designed to be parallel to the confluence of the <a href="/wiki/South_Platte_River" title="South Platte River">South Platte River</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cherry_Creek_(Colorado)" title="Cherry Creek (Colorado)">Cherry Creek</a>. Most of the streets downtown and in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LoDo" title="LoDo">LoDo</a> run northeast–southwest and northwest–southeast. This system has an unplanned benefit for snow removal; if the streets were in a normal N–S/E–W grid, only the N–S streets would receive sunlight. With the grid oriented to the diagonal directions, the NW–SE streets receive sunlight to melt snow in the morning and the NE–SW streets receive it in the afternoon. This idea was from Henry Brown the founder of the <a href="/wiki/Brown_Palace_Hotel_(Denver,_Colorado)" title="Brown Palace Hotel (Denver, Colorado)">Brown Palace Hotel</a>. There is now a plaque across the street from the <a href="/wiki/Brown_Palace_Hotel_(Denver,_Colorado)" title="Brown Palace Hotel (Denver, Colorado)">Brown Palace Hotel</a> which honors this idea. The NW–SE streets are numbered, while the NE–SW streets are named. The named streets start at the intersection of Colfax Avenue and Broadway with the block-long Cheyenne Place. The numbered streets start underneath the Colfax and I-25 viaducts. There are 27 named and 44 numbered streets on this grid. There are also a few vestiges of the old grid system in the normal grid, such as Park Avenue, Morrison Road, and Speer Boulevard. Larimer Street, named after <a href="/wiki/William_Larimer,_Jr." title="William Larimer, Jr.">William Larimer, Jr.</a>, the founder of Denver, which is located in the heart of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LoDo" title="LoDo">LoDo</a>, is the oldest street in Denver.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Speer_Boulevard_Denver.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2039" data-file-width="2243" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Speer_Boulevard_Denver.JPG/220px-Speer_Boulevard_Denver.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Speer_Boulevard_Denver.JPG/330px-Speer_Boulevard_Denver.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Speer_Boulevard_Denver.JPG/440px-Speer_Boulevard_Denver.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Speer_Boulevard_Denver.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Speer Blvd runs north and south through downtown Denver</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>All roads in the downtown grid system are streets (e.g. <a href="/wiki/16th_Street_Mall" title="16th Street Mall">16th Street</a>, Stout Street). Roads outside that system that travel east/west are given the suffix "avenue" and those that head north and south are given the "street" suffix (e.g. Colfax Avenue, Lincoln Street). Boulevards are higher capacity streets and travel any direction (more commonly north and south). Smaller roads are sometimes referred to as places, drives (though not all drives are smaller capacity roads, some are major thoroughfares) or courts. Most streets outside the area between Broadway and Colorado Boulevard are organized alphabetically from the city's center.</p>
<p>Many Denver streets have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bicycle_lanes" title="Bicycle lanes">bicycle lanes</a>, and there are over 850 miles<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-denver.org_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-denver.org-129">[127]</a></sup> of paved, off-road, bike paths in Denver parks and along bodies of water, like Cherry Creek and the South Platte. This allows for a significant portion of Denver's population to be bicycle commuters and has led to Denver being known as a bicycle friendly city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-League_of_American_Bicylistsl_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-League_of_American_Bicylistsl-130">[128]</a></sup> In addition to the many bike paths, Denver launched B-Cycle – a citywide bicycle sharing program – in late April 2010. The B-Cycle network was the largest in the United States at the time of its launch, boasting 400 bicycles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cnet_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cnet-131">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Wheel_clamp" title="Wheel clamp">Denver Boot</a>, a car-disabling device was first used in Denver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[130]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cycling">Cycling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Cycling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The League of American Bicyclists has rated Colorado as the sixth most bicycle-friendly state in the nation for the year 2014. This is due in large part to Front Range cities like Boulder, Fort Collins and Denver placing an emphasis on legislation, programs and infrastructure developments that promote cycling as a mode of transportation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[131]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Walk_Score" title="Walk Score">Walk score</a> has rated Denver as the third most bicycle-friendly large city in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[132]</a></sup> According to data from the 2011 American Community Survey, Denver ranks 6th among US cities with populations over 400,000 in terms of the percentage of <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._cities_with_most_bicycle_commuters" title="List of U.S. cities with most bicycle commuters">workers who commute by bicycle</a> at 2.2% of commuters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[133]</a></sup> B-Cycle – Denver's citywide bicycle sharing program – was the largest in the United States at the time of its launch, boasting 400 bicycles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cnet_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cnet-131">[129]</a></sup> Through the acquisition of new grants, the program has continued to expand each year, adding dozens of new stations, hundreds of bikes, and by beginning service during the winter months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[135]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Walkability">Walkability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Walkability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A 2011 study by <a href="/wiki/Walk_Score" title="Walk Score">Walk Score</a> ranked Denver sixteenth most walkable of fifty largest U.S. cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[136]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Freeways_and_highways">Freeways and highways</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Freeways and highways">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Denver is primarily served by the interstate freeways <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstate_25_(Colorado)" title="Interstate 25 (Colorado)">I-25</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstate_70_(Colorado)" title="Interstate 70 (Colorado)">I-70</a>. The intersection of the two interstates is referred to locally as "<a href="/wiki/Mousetrap_(Denver)" title="Mousetrap (Denver)">the mousetrap</a>", because when viewed from the air, the junction (and subsequent vehicles) resemble mice in a large trap.</p>
<ul>
<li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:I-25_(CO).svg"><img alt="I-25 (CO).svg" data-file-height="601" data-file-width="601" height="25" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/I-25_%28CO%29.svg/25px-I-25_%28CO%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/I-25_%28CO%29.svg/38px-I-25_%28CO%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/I-25_%28CO%29.svg/50px-I-25_%28CO%29.svg.png 2x" width="25"/></a> <b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstate_25_(Colorado)" title="Interstate 25 (Colorado)">Interstate 25</a></b> runs north–south from <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a> through Denver to <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a></li>
<li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:I-225_(CO).svg"><img alt="I-225 (CO).svg" data-file-height="601" data-file-width="751" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/I-225_%28CO%29.svg/25px-I-225_%28CO%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/I-225_%28CO%29.svg/38px-I-225_%28CO%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/I-225_%28CO%29.svg/50px-I-225_%28CO%29.svg.png 2x" width="25"/></a> <b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstate_225_(Colorado)" title="Interstate 225 (Colorado)">Interstate 225</a></b> traverses neighboring Aurora. I-225 was designed to link Aurora with I-25 in the southeastern corner of Denver, and I-70 to the north of Aurora, with construction starting May 1964 and ending May 21, 1976.</li>
<li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:I-70_(CO).svg"><img alt="I-70 (CO).svg" data-file-height="601" data-file-width="601" height="25" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/I-70_%28CO%29.svg/25px-I-70_%28CO%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/I-70_%28CO%29.svg/38px-I-70_%28CO%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/I-70_%28CO%29.svg/50px-I-70_%28CO%29.svg.png 2x" width="25"/></a> <b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstate_70_(Colorado)" title="Interstate 70 (Colorado)">Interstate 70</a></b> runs east–west from <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a> to <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>.</li>
<li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:I-270_(CO).svg"><img alt="I-270 (CO).svg" data-file-height="601" data-file-width="751" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/I-270_%28CO%29.svg/25px-I-270_%28CO%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/I-270_%28CO%29.svg/38px-I-270_%28CO%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/I-270_%28CO%29.svg/50px-I-270_%28CO%29.svg.png 2x" width="25"/></a> <b><a href="/wiki/Interstate_270_(Colorado)" title="Interstate 270 (Colorado)">Interstate 270</a></b> runs concurrently with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Highway_36_(Colorado)" title="U.S. Highway 36 (Colorado)">US 36</a> from an interchange with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstate_70_(Colorado)" title="Interstate 70 (Colorado)">Interstate 70</a> in northeast Denver to an interchange with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstate_25_(Colorado)" title="Interstate 25 (Colorado)">Interstate 25</a> north of Denver. The freeway continues as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Highway_36_(Colorado)" title="U.S. Highway 36 (Colorado)">US 36</a> from the interchange with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstate_25_(Colorado)" title="Interstate 25 (Colorado)">Interstate 25</a>.</li>
<li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:I-76_(CO).svg"><img alt="I-76 (CO).svg" data-file-height="601" data-file-width="601" height="25" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/I-76_%28CO%29.svg/25px-I-76_%28CO%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/I-76_%28CO%29.svg/38px-I-76_%28CO%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/I-76_%28CO%29.svg/50px-I-76_%28CO%29.svg.png 2x" width="25"/></a> <b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstate_76_(Colorado)" title="Interstate 76 (Colorado)">Interstate 76</a></b> begins from I-70 just west of the city in <a href="/wiki/Arvada,_Colorado" title="Arvada, Colorado">Arvada</a>. It intersects I-25 north of the city and runs northeast to Nebraska where it ends at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstate_80_(Nebraska)" title="Interstate 80 (Nebraska)">I-80</a>.</li>
<li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_6.svg"><img alt="US 6.svg" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" height="25" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/US_6.svg/25px-US_6.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/US_6.svg/38px-US_6.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/US_6.svg/50px-US_6.svg.png 2x" width="25"/></a> <b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Highway_6_(Colorado)" title="U.S. Highway 6 (Colorado)">US 6</a></b> follows the alignment of 6th Avenue west of I-25, and connects downtown Denver to the west-central suburbs of <a href="/wiki/Golden,_Colorado" title="Golden, Colorado">Golden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lakewood,_Colorado" title="Lakewood, Colorado">Lakewood</a>. It continues west through Utah and Nevada to Bishop, California. To the east, it continues as far as Provincetown, on Cape Cod in Massachusetts.</li>
<li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_36.svg"><img alt="US 36.svg" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" height="25" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/US_36.svg/25px-US_36.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/US_36.svg/38px-US_36.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/US_36.svg/50px-US_36.svg.png 2x" width="25"/></a> <b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Highway_36_(Colorado)" title="U.S. Highway 36 (Colorado)">US 36</a></b> connects Denver to <a href="/wiki/Boulder,_Colorado" title="Boulder, Colorado">Boulder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountain_National_Park" title="Rocky Mountain National Park">Rocky Mountain National Park</a> near <a href="/wiki/Estes_Park,_Colorado" title="Estes Park, Colorado">Estes Park</a>. It runs east into <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a>, after crossing four other states.</li>
<li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Colorado_470.svg"><img alt="Colorado 470.svg" data-file-height="601" data-file-width="601" height="25" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Colorado_470.svg/25px-Colorado_470.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Colorado_470.svg/38px-Colorado_470.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Colorado_470.svg/50px-Colorado_470.svg.png 2x" width="25"/></a><b>State Highway 470</b> (<b>C-470</b>, <b>SH 470</b>) is the southwestern portion of the Denver metro area's beltway. Originally planned as Interstate 470 in the 1960s, the beltway project was attacked on environmental impact grounds and the interstate beltway was never built. The portion of "Interstate 470" that was built as a <a href="/wiki/State_highway" title="State highway">state highway</a> is the present-day SH 470, which is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freeway" title="Freeway">freeway</a> for its entire length.</li>
</ul>
<p>Denver also has a nearly complete beltway known as "the 470's". These are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SH_470_(CO)" title="SH 470 (CO)">SH 470</a> (also known as C-470), a freeway in the southwest Metro area, and two toll highways, <a href="/wiki/E-470" title="E-470">E-470</a> (from southeast to northeast) and <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Parkway" title="Northwest Parkway">Northwest Parkway</a> (from terminus of E-470 to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Highway_36_(Colorado)" title="U.S. Highway 36 (Colorado)">US 36</a>). SH 470 was originally intended to be I-470 and built with federal highway funds, but the funding was redirected to complete conversion of downtown Denver's <a href="/wiki/16th_Street_Mall" title="16th Street Mall">16th Street</a> to a pedestrian mall. As a result, construction was delayed until 1980 after state and local legislation was passed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[137]</a></sup> I-470 was also once called "The Silver Stake Highway", from Gov. Lamm's declared intention to drive a silver stake through it and kill it.</p>
<p>A highway expansion and transit project for the southern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstate_25_(Colorado)" title="Interstate 25 (Colorado)">I-25</a> corridor, dubbed T-REX (<a href="/wiki/Transportation_Expansion_Project" title="Transportation Expansion Project">Transportation Expansion Project</a>), was completed on November 17, 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[138]</a></sup> The project installed wider and additional highway lanes, and improved highway access and drainage. The project also includes a light rail line that traverses from downtown to the south end of the metro area at Lincoln Avenue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[139]</a></sup> The project spanned almost 19 miles (31 km) along the highway with an additional line traveling parallel to part of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstate_225_(Colorado)" title="Interstate 225 (Colorado)">I-225</a>, stopping just short of Parker Road.</p>
<p>Metro Denver highway conditions can be accessed on the <a href="/wiki/Colorado_Department_of_Transportation" title="Colorado Department of Transportation">Colorado Department of Transportation</a> website Traffic Conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[140]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mass_transportation">Mass transportation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Denver&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Mass transportation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Colfaxatauraria.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3456" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Colfaxatauraria.JPG/220px-Colfaxatauraria.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Colfaxatauraria.JPG/330px-Colfaxatauraria.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Colfaxatauraria.JPG/440px-Colfaxatauraria.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
